  //<![CDATA[
    function loadScript(url, callback) {
      var script = document.createElement("script");
      script.type = "text/javascript";
    
      if (script.readyState) {  //Internet Explorer
          script.onreadystatechange = function() {
            if (script.readyState == "loaded" ||
                    script.readyState == "complete") {
              script.onreadystatechange = null;
              callback();
            }
          };
      } else {  //Other browsers
        script.onload = function() {
          callback();
        };
      }
    
      script.src = url;
      document.getElementsByTagName("head")[0].appendChild(script);
    }
    
    function initAppleId() {
      AppleID.auth.init({
        clientId : 'com.goodreads.app', 
        scope : 'name email',
        redirectURI: 'https://www.goodreads.com/apple_users/sign_in_with_apple_web',
        state: 'apple_oauth_state_af55ec2e-56c2-4174-8faa-1c353ba57bc9'
      });
    }

<span id="freeTextContainer8278650483833968799">From #1 <i>New York Times</i> bestselling author George R. R. Martin comes the ultimate novel of revolution, rock ’n’ roll, and apocalyptic murder—a stunning work of fiction that portrays not just the end of an era, but the end of the world as we know it.<br/><br/>Onetime underground journalist Sandy Blair has come a long way from his radical roots in the ’60s—until something unexpectedly </span>
  <span id="freeText8278650483833968799" style="display:none">From #1 <i>New York Times</i> bestselling author George R. R. Martin comes the ultimate novel of revolution, rock ’n’ roll, and apocalyptic murder—a stunning work of fiction that portrays not just the end of an era, but the end of the world as we know it.<br /><br />Onetime underground journalist Sandy Blair has come a long way from his radical roots in the ’60s—until something unexpectedly draws him back: the bizarre and brutal murder of a rock promoter who made millions with a band called the Nazgûl. Now, as Sandy sets out to investigate the crime, he finds himself drawn back into his own past—a magical mystery tour of the pent-up passions of his generation. For a new messiah has resurrected the Nazgûl and the mad new rhythm may be more than anyone bargained for—a requiem of demonism, mind control, and death, whose apocalyptic tune only Sandy may be able to change in time . . . before everyone follows the beat.</span>

    <div class="section firstReview">
      

<div id="review_1067811053" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1067811053" />
    <a title="Jack Tripper" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/35473855-jack-tripper"><img alt="Jack Tripper" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1484547420p2/35473855.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1067811053?book_show_action=true">Sep 29, 2014</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Jack Tripper" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Jack Tripper" href="/user/show/35473855-jack-tripper">Jack Tripper</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780671532536" href="/book/show/1170482.The_Armageddon_Rag">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/35473855-jack-tripper?shelf=horror">horror</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/35473855-jack-tripper?shelf=mystery-crime-thriller">mystery-crime-thriller</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1067811053" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer15726056047984895473">George RR Martin was rising up the ranks in sf/f and horror in the early 80s, winning awards left and right. Coming off his critically-acclaimed <i>Sandkings</i> collection and the highly successful historical vampire thriller, <i>Fevre Dream</i>, Martin's publisher rightfully pushed <i>The Armageddon Rag</i> as "the next big thing." Only no one bought it, and as a result the emotionally crushed author basically left the world of fiction-writing to become a Hollywood screenwriter instead.<br/><br/>At the time, this was Martin</span>
  <span id="freeText15726056047984895473" style="display:none">George RR Martin was rising up the ranks in sf/f and horror in the early 80s, winning awards left and right. Coming off his critically-acclaimed <i>Sandkings</i> collection and the highly successful historical vampire thriller, <i>Fevre Dream</i>, Martin's publisher rightfully pushed <i>The Armageddon Rag</i> as "the next big thing." Only no one bought it, and as a result the emotionally crushed author basically left the world of fiction-writing to become a Hollywood screenwriter instead.<br /><br />At the time, this was Martin's most ambitious work yet, and one that was very close to his heart, he being a child of the 60s. Drawing from his own personal experiences of the Summer of Love-era, this supernatural horror/thriller tells the tale of Sandy, a rock journalist in the early 80s who's investigating the mysterious, gruesome murder of a promoter who managed several big-name bands throughout the 60s and 70s, including hard-rockers The Nazgûl, one of the most famous of the era. And they broke up exactly 10 years before to the day, when their frontman was murdered. Fearing a connection, Sandy decides to visit some old friends from back in the day, including the remaining Nazgûl members, to see if he can get to the bottom of it all. <br /><br />Much of the novel is just an excuse for Martin to reminisce about the good ole' flower-power days, as that's what main character Sandy does throughout the first half, while reconnecting with his old buddies. It's very well-written, but it takes a while to get to the more interesting, fantastical elements of the story, and I had a hard time keeping my focus during the first half. But it does shift to supernatural horror in a clever way that I won't describe, for fear of ruining it for others. <br /><br />Overall, it's not something I'd go out of my way to hunt down, but if you come across it for a decent price, it may be worth a look-see. But if you're really interested in reading some of Martin's early horror work, start with his excellent collections like <i>Sandkings</i> or <i>Nightflyers</i> first*.<br /><br />3.0 Stars<br /><br />*The title stories of each collection are two of the most chilling sf/horror hybrids I've read. "Sandkings" is around novelette-length (47 pages in my edition), while "Nightflyers" could almost be considered a short novel (over 100 pages). Both can be more easily found in Martin's <i>Dreamsongs</i> retrospective.</span>
  <a data-text-id="15726056047984895473" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">

<span id="freeTextContainer6386387560060870399">I read this book at the urging of a number of people, since I write a lot of music-infused SFF and this was a rock n roll fantasy novel. It gets a solid three stars. Very readable, cinematic even. Martin does write music well, and this hits some fine creepy notes. It's an interesting look at the 60s and 70s from the not very far out early 80s. That said, I found the portrayals of women frustrating. </span>

<div id="review_571649971" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/571649971" />
    <a title="Kara Babcock" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/1188122-kara-babcock"><img alt="Kara Babcock" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1580776593p2/1188122.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/571649971?book_show_action=true">Mar 26, 2013</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Kara Babcock" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Kara Babcock" href="/user/show/1188122-kara-babcock">Kara Babcock</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780575129559" href="/book/show/17270585-the-armageddon-rag">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1188122-kara-babcock?shelf=2013-read">2013-read</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1188122-kara-babcock?shelf=fantasy">fantasy</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1188122-kara-babcock?shelf=urban-fantasy">urban-fantasy</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1188122-kara-babcock?shelf=from-library">from-library</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1188122-kara-babcock?shelf=mystery">mystery</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer571649971" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer16985976690297082092">Did you know George R.R. Martin wrote novels <em>before</em> <em>A Game of Thrones</em>? Yes, it’s true! And you can read them! On paper, even! <em>The Armageddon Rag</em> is a 1980s tale of a journalist-turned-novelist recapturing the zeitgeist of the 1970s music scene. Spurred by a mysterious, sacrificial killing of a music promoter, Sandy Blair discovers that there might be more to it. Someone has a plan to reunite the band Nazgûl—particularly troubling since its lead singer is dead.<br/><br/>Sandy leaves the adult world of resp</span>
  <span id="freeText16985976690297082092" style="display:none">Did you know George R.R. Martin wrote novels <em>before</em> <em>A Game of Thrones</em>? Yes, it’s true! And you can read them! On paper, even! <em>The Armageddon Rag</em> is a 1980s tale of a journalist-turned-novelist recapturing the zeitgeist of the 1970s music scene. Spurred by a mysterious, sacrificial killing of a music promoter, Sandy Blair discovers that there might be more to it. Someone has a plan to reunite the band Nazgûl—particularly troubling since its lead singer is dead.<br><br>Sandy leaves the adult world of responsibility behind and goes off on a cross-country road trip to track down the surviving members of the Nazgûl. Along the way, he visits several friends with whom he has lost touch—members of the revolutionary circles in which he moved when he was younger. The trappings of Sandy’s present fall by the wayside in favour of continuing to recapture his present. As he continues to investigate the killing of Jamie Lynch, Sandy discovers that there is a supernatural element to his news story. And it might just eat his soul.<br><br><em>The Armageddon Rag</em> is a very different beast from <em>A Song of Ice and Fire</em>, but they do share one thing: both are ambivalently supernatural at first. It’s not clear, at the beginning of this book, whether there is a supernatural element to the crime or merely the appearance of one. GRRM teases us, dangling the possibility of magic but never quite confirming it. He fakes us out a few times—the seemingly-impossible re-emergence of Patrick Hobson is one example. It’s not until the second half of the book, as Sandy’s sanity, steeped in the atmosphere of the renewed Nazgûl, begins to unravel.<br><br>So for the majority of the book, this is a music murder mystery. Sandy is an unlikely detective (GRRM hangs a lampshade on this through Sandy’s own reflections). However, it’s fair to say that the story isn’t about the mystery as much as it is about the music, and the relationship between music, culture, and the revolution that Sandy held so dear in his younger days. To this end, Sandy’s various reunions with his old pals provide great insight into how he has changed in response to the decline of that revolutionary attitude. Each of his friends has reacted to that decline in different ways. Maggie has clung to her old lifestyle, attempting to remain carefree. Lark—restyled as L. Steven Elleyn—has embraced the suit-and-tie atmosphere of middle management. Bambi has stuck her head in the sand and joined a commune. And Sandy, of course, quickly discovers that he isn’t quite so adult and settled as his life as a writer and boyfriend might make him appear.<br><br>I’m actually rather surprised by how much I enjoyed <em>The Armageddon Rag</em>. So much of it takes place—or is influenced by the atmosphere of—those “lost decades” of history for me, the 1960s, 1970s, and 1980s. Too recent to have received much coverage in history classes; too distant to have the same connection to me as the 1990s and 2000s, those decades seem forever just out of reach. I can’t identify with Sandy the way an older reader might. So it’s a testament to GRRM’s skill that I still understood and sympathized with this journey of rediscovery, which ultimately culminates in Sandy abandoning his journalistic endeavour to do public relations for the Nazgûl.<br><br>The tone of the book begins to shift, with supernatural elements coming to the fore. Sandy begins to realize that the reunion of the Nazgûl, the “resurrection” of Patrick Hobson, their new concert at West Mesa, are all part of a larger plan. Trust someone like GRRM to come up with the idea that Judgement Day will take the form of a concert! Despite the apocalyptic angle, however, the climax of the book isn’t so much about the battle between good and evil as the battle between Sandy’s sense of self-determination and his commitment to the “cause”. His entire journey is an examination of whether he abandoned the “revolution” because he wasn’t committed enough. His crucial decision at the climax of the concert is the last word.<br><br><em>The Armageddon Rag</em> has moments of brilliance. Its supernatural elements aren’t quite married with my own tastes in this genre, and for that reason I can’t give it five stars. But the mystery, story, and especially the characterization are all what one would expect from GRRM. It’s not enough to tide me over until the next <em>Song of Ice and Fire</em> book … but it helps.<br><br><a href="http://creativecommons.org/licenses/by-nc/3.0/" rel="nofollow noopener">
  <img alt="Creative Commons BY-NC License" width="88" height="31" src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1380238653i/681550.png" class="gr-hostedUserImg">
</a></span>
  <a data-text-id="16985976690297082092" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

      <span class='likeItContainer' id='like_container_review_571649971'><a id="like_count_review_571649971" rel="nofollow" href="/rating/voters/571649971?resource_type=Review"><span class="likesCount">8 likes</span></a>&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;<a rel="nofollow" class="jsLike like_it gr-button gr-button--small" href="https://www.goodreads.com/user/new?remember=true&amp;return_url=%2Fbook%2Fshow%2F67948.The_Armageddon_Rag">Like</a></span>

  <div class="friendReviews elementListBrown" >
    <div class="section">
      

<div id="review_844299723" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/844299723" />
    <a title="Sherwood Smith" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/162084-sherwood-smith"><img alt="Sherwood Smith" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1543007644p2/162084.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/844299723?book_show_action=true">Feb 04, 2014</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Sherwood Smith" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Sherwood Smith" href="/user/show/162084-sherwood-smith">Sherwood Smith</a>
      </span>

          added it




        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/162084-sherwood-smith?shelf=fantasy">fantasy</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer844299723" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer15891457969114693279">Youth, anger, and rock and roll—three things with the power of magic, especially for those of us who were young in the sixties. In this combination murder mystery and road trip novel, Martin evokes that vividly, and then ponders where it all went.<br/><br/>The opening swiftly sets the scene: as the hippie generation swelled into student protest in the late sixties, the rock band called Nazgûl became the voice of a generation. Their rise to fame peaked on September 20th, 1971, at an enormous outdoor concer</span>
  <span id="freeText15891457969114693279" style="display:none">Youth, anger, and rock and roll—three things with the power of magic, especially for those of us who were young in the sixties. In this combination murder mystery and road trip novel, Martin evokes that vividly, and then ponders where it all went.<br /><br />The opening swiftly sets the scene: as the hippie generation swelled into student protest in the late sixties, the rock band called Nazgûl became the voice of a generation. Their rise to fame peaked on September 20th, 1971, at an enormous outdoor concert in West Mesa, New Mexico, then abruptly fell with the shooting death of the lead singer, Patrick Henry Hobbins, or ‘Hobbit,” right on stage.<br /><br />The murder remained unsolved and the band went their separate ways. Their followers also dispersed, including Sandy Blair, a young student-journalist who’d helped found a rock magazine called Hedgehog. He wrote for the Hog until he was fired. The Hog was going mainstream, where the money was, leaving Sandy in the wreckage of the Age of Aquarius.<br /><br />Now Blair is a novelist whose three publications have been progressively less successful. Stuck on page 37, he’s living with a real estate agent whose grownup attitude has pulled him into a practical, contract-defined, gender-equal condo that is a creative black hole. <br /><br />He gets a call from his ex-friend and editor at the Hog to investigate the murder of the rock agent who had once managed the Nazgûl. Blair is curious as well as at loose ends; he drives to Maine to discover that the murder was carried out in an extremely grisly ritual, with visual evidence linking to one of the Nazgûls’ songs.<br /><br />His literary agent and his girlfriend both want him to man up, drop the investigation and get back to practical matters. His response? What is the only possible response when everyone around you earnestly advises you to grow up and face reality? Road trip! <br /><br />As Blair travels cross country to interview former Nazgûl and his once-activist friends, he is also traveling into his past. But the present doesn’t stand still: violence follows him, making it clear that the murder of the rock agent is no isolated incident, but part of a pattern.<br /><br />The trip into memory, fuel-injected by rock lyrics of the sixties, shifts into psychedelic fantasy, as a new impresario who fought for the sixties revolution brings the band together, the goal a repeat performance at West Mesa. Sandy, the emotional, political, and spiritual agnostic, hired as reluctant publicity flack, becomes the key player in an apocalypse that he doesn’t believe he’s seeing until he’s got the past, present, and future in the cross-hairs.<br /><br />As I was rereading the book in the handsome new Gollancz edition, the cover caught the eye of my son. The art depicts the Eye of Sauron set in the scope of a sniper rifle, and Martin’s name is even larger than the title. My son is the drummer in a rock band; as a boy, he sat beside me each December when the latest Tolkien Ring film came out; and while I don’t know if he’s watching the <i>Game of Thrones</i> fantasy series on TV, he certainly recognized Martin’s name. <br /><br />I thought he’d be the perfect reader for this book, and began describing it. As I did, I watched his reaction, aware of the paradigmatic divide between my generation and his. When I read Tolkien’s <i>Lord of the Rings</i> as a teen in the mid-sixties, it belonged to people my age, the counter-culture. I found it incredibly exciting, almost a secret code, when <i>Lord of the Rings</i> references began showing up in rock songs and on posters. <br /><br />For my son, <i>Lord of the Rings</i> was Stuff Mom and Dad read, made into films that everybody went to see. Counter-culture for him in his mid-teens was punk rock and art, maybe <i>Sandman</i> comics. The sixties to him meant boring lectures and old vids about flag burning and the Viet Nam War, Nixon and Watergate . . . test questions on history exams, not the blood, sweat and tears of people once his own age who thought to reshape the world, inspired—sometimes driven—by music. <br /><br /><i>Armageddon Rag</i> was first published in 1983, three years after Reagan’s election. Sandy Blair symbolizes so many of the thirty-something ex-revolutionary baby boomers, so many of whom were bitter, bewildered, wondering what had happened to the revolution, and how could we end up with a President who made Tricky Dick look liberal? <br /><br />Reading that book in the eighties, I found Sandy’s existential angst, the sky-challenging What happened to us? resonated so deeply that I could scarcely bear to reread the novel. Images from it chased me for quite a while after, and friends and I who talked about the novel often ruminated about which band’s songs were closest to the “Rag.” <br /><br />In the mid-eighties, as I recall, the most agreed-on songs were “Inna-gadda-da-vida” and the long version of “Stairway to Heaven,” but on this reread what came to mind was a vid I saw a year or two ago of a performance by Schandmaul—whose rock demonstrates a strong Tolkienian influence—when thousands of their fans took over chanting one of the lines and wouldn’t let it go. The exhilaration teetering on the edge of mass action seemed to disconcert the band, to put it mildly.<br /><br />It’s that eerie reality-distorting vibe that turns crowds into mobs with a single mind and a single goal that Martin evokes so effectively. I used the term psychedelic fantasy up above because Blair is never really certain that the supernatural has irrupted into the natural world as he defines it, or if he’s hallucinating. <br /><br />On this reread, I found the earnest world-weariness of a bunch of thirty-somethings was amusing to me in my sixties, further, that some of Sandy’s anxious ranting about what went wrong tended toward length and repetition. Whenever Sandy starts shaking, you know several pages of tirade are coming up, some more effective than others: on page 60, when he cries, “I don’t like it, I don’t like it!” and two lines later he blames the wine, I was thinking, unfortunate choice of words. I didn’t need the echo of “whine.” <br /><br />Another aspect that had decidedly dated was the cheerfully unconscious (white) male het view of women: while the men get generally described, the women all receive visual reports on the size and shape of their breasts. Though Martin plays fair for the modern reader, in that Sandy does not get a free pass for his sexually-oriented evaluations of character.<br /> <br />The action rises to a dramatic climax, but on this reread, the scene I found the most effective was Sandy’s plunge among the ghosts of the disastrous 1968 Democratic National Convention in Chicago. Occult-caused time travel?—actual ghosts?—the characters’ differing perceptions of reality overlap at times like interlocking war zones. Whose version of reality is ‘right’ is immaterial, even uninteresting, juxtaposed against the resonance of truth.<br /><br />And it’s exactly this aspect (as well as the book being overall a ripping good yarn) that I’d love to talk over with my son. It is inevitable that we are going to look at human behavior, history, and music from either end of the decades in question, but one thing for certain: any book that can foster such discussion and still entertain, no matter what the age of the reader, is a keeper.<br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="15891457969114693279" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<tr class="no_border feedFooterReview" id="update_comment_stuff_Review844299723">

<div id="review_2208956890" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2208956890" />
    <a title="Silvana" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/666003-silvana"><img alt="Silvana" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1600533521p2/666003.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2208956890?book_show_action=true">Sep 02, 2018</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Silvana" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Silvana" href="/user/show/666003-silvana">Silvana</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780575129559" href="/book/show/17270585-the-armageddon-rag">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/666003-silvana?shelf=fyeahfantasy">fyeahfantasy</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/666003-silvana?shelf=z-sl-sword-challenge-2018">z-sl-sword-challenge-2018</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/666003-silvana?shelf=reading-with-friends">reading-with-friends</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/666003-silvana?shelf=mine-mine-mine">mine-mine-mine</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/666003-silvana?shelf=dead-trees">dead-trees</a>
          </div>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2208956890" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer17009926930834785782">George R.R Martin knows how to write songs. If you read the ASOIAF novels, you'll know some memorable songs like the ominous 'The Rains of Castamere', the tragic 'The Last of the Giants', or the bawdy 'The Bear and the Maiden Fair'. In The Armageddon Rag, where the story revolves around this Led Zeppelin-like band, you'll find many songs too. The way George writes about the performance of each song will make you feel you're seeing them live. In fact, as my BR friend Siobhan said in her <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2054333151' rel='nofollow noopener'>excellent</a></span>
  <span id="freeText17009926930834785782" style="display:none">George R.R Martin knows how to write songs. If you read the ASOIAF novels, you'll know some memorable songs like the ominous 'The Rains of Castamere', the tragic 'The Last of the Giants', or the bawdy 'The Bear and the Maiden Fair'. In The Armageddon Rag, where the story revolves around this Led Zeppelin-like band, you'll find many songs too. The way George writes about the performance of each song will make you feel you're seeing them live. In fact, as my BR friend Siobhan said in her <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2054333151" rel="nofollow noopener">excellent review</a> it makes the readers want to buy their album. <br /><br />Yes, we want The Nazgul (yes, inspired by <i>that</i> one) to be a real band because we have become so emotionally invested in their journey from the revolutionary 60s to the 80s. It shows how good and immersive George's writing is.<br /><br />The real rating is 4.5 stars. I have to round it down because I'd like to have more explanation on the fantastical side of it. But, still, this a book that will stay with both of us for a long time. If you're a fan of ASOIAF and want to get a feel of his other works, do try this one, I guarantee you can get satisfaction. *wink*</span>

<span id="freeTextContainer5089043920702585940">Okokokok, I love me some George RR Martin, but this book is severely lacking. It is by far the worst I've read from him and in it he accomplishes something I never thought could be possible... he writes some very one dimensional characters!<br/><br/>The story is a gigantic leap from the song of ice and fire world and isn't as cleverly crafted as the fevre dream world. The characters in this book are unlikable and stale. The main protagonist is a close minded, hippie, doucebag journalist who is obsessed wi</span>
  <span id="freeText5089043920702585940" style="display:none">Okokokok, I love me some George RR Martin, but this book is severely lacking. It is by far the worst I've read from him and in it he accomplishes something I never thought could be possible... he writes some very one dimensional characters!<br /><br />The story is a gigantic leap from the song of ice and fire world and isn't as cleverly crafted as the fevre dream world. The characters in this book are unlikable and stale. The main protagonist is a close minded, hippie, doucebag journalist who is obsessed with his past glory days. I was debating whether I should quit the book about 100 pages in, but I stuck it out because I really wanted to see if he would write a happy or a sad ending, to give me an idea of what's to come in ASOIAF. The story is unbelievable and tacky and the rock and rollers are over the top stereotypes. It's as if Martin had never talked to a musician in his life or something. <br /><br /><br />It had some interesting ideas and cool parts, but overall, it was a gigantic letdown that I wouldn't recommend to even some of the most hardcore Martin fans.<br /><br />3/10</span>

    <a title="Siobhan" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/35855553-siobhan"><img alt="Siobhan" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1510311895p2/35855553.jpg" /></a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Siobhan" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Siobhan" href="/user/show/35855553-siobhan">Siobhan</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9781407247281" href="/book/show/21532697-the-armageddon-rag">review of another edition</a>


        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1086880807" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer5117678420298081502">I really wanted to enjoy this one more than I did.<br/><br/>I love Martin’s work. Don’t get me wrong, I do not have the most extensive Martin collection, but I have read a decent number. Sadly, however, this one makes the cut as my least favourite George R. R. Martin book. Such a thing really disappoints me as I’d been sure that it would win me over in the end.<br/><br/>Alas, this book was made up of moments where I was really enjoying it and moments where I just wanted the whole thing to be over. To make matters w</span>
  <span id="freeText5117678420298081502" style="display:none">I really wanted to enjoy this one more than I did.<br /><br />I love Martin’s work. Don’t get me wrong, I do not have the most extensive Martin collection, but I have read a decent number. Sadly, however, this one makes the cut as my least favourite George R. R. Martin book. Such a thing really disappoints me as I’d been sure that it would win me over in the end.<br /><br />Alas, this book was made up of moments where I was really enjoying it and moments where I just wanted the whole thing to be over. To make matters worse, there were more of the latter. Such a thing makes it hard for me to gauge just how much I enjoyed this read. It’s a case of when I was enjoying it I was really enjoying it but when I wasn’t I completely hated it. The shift between the two stages was so rapid, with the hatred stages lasting longer, and now a part of me feels as though I have betrayed Martin by disliking this book.<br /><br />The idea was great, it had the potential to be such an interesting story, yet it did not cut it for me. In fact, I felt as though there was quite a bit of false advertisement with this one. It seemed more like a trip down memory lane for our main character than it did the following of some strange apocalyptic band. It was a weird read, but not the right kind of weird.<br /><br />Overall, I’d simply been expecting more.</span>
  <a data-text-id="5117678420298081502" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_343381716" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/343381716" />
    <a title="Richard Sutton" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/2923014-richard-sutton"><img alt="Richard Sutton" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1257716706p2/2923014.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/343381716?book_show_action=true">Jun 06, 2012</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Richard Sutton" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Richard Sutton" href="/user/show/2923014-richard-sutton">Richard Sutton</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer343381716" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer5965992725711357035">Writers usually have a few stories that bounce around in their heads for years. Some of them eventually make it to the page, while others just circulate and create occasional moodiness or anxiety behind the scenes. As a bonafide, ex-commune hippie... politically, a real "man the barricades" kind of guy, vague feelings of guilt over how I, and my generation, seem to have lost the ideals that seemed so important back then have been circulating for years. I want to sincerely thank George Martin for</span>
  <span id="freeText5965992725711357035" style="display:none">Writers usually have a few stories that bounce around in their heads for years. Some of them eventually make it to the page, while others just circulate and create occasional moodiness or anxiety behind the scenes. As a bonafide, ex-commune hippie... politically, a real "man the barricades" kind of guy, vague feelings of guilt over how I, and my generation, seem to have lost the ideals that seemed so important back then have been circulating for years. I want to sincerely thank George Martin for this book. He has saved me the trouble of trying to confront these issues myself on paper and deal with the fallout.<br /><br />The Armageddon Rag is a fast-paced mystery, but also a well-tuned personal introspection as the main character sorts through his feelings of "where did it all go?". I identified seamlessly with Sandy, his writer/movement reporter, as he comes to terms with how he fits into the swirling emotions and lost friendships that twenty years can bring as well as a horrific murder. The author uses the device of music lyrics and the music scene of the time to create a plausible connection with occult power and ghostly hordes, driven towards a final battle. He has to make peace with his own demons in order to be able to confront the actions of the demons that may be forcing mankind into perpetual violence and anguish. As a musician, also, I've found myself ascribing earth-changing power to the songs I grew up with, just as Sandy does.<br /><br />The release date for this book, in 1982, was also important for me in understanding the character's anger and confusion as well as cultural perspectives that only age can understand. The writer's gentle affection for the tortured souls he writes about here (and in all his later work as well),left this reader with a distinct sense of redemption at the end of the book. Peace and Love and Brotherhood weren't just buzzwords, and they haven't died. They're waiting to be picked up and dusted off.<br /><br />In all, for someone who maybe close to or a bit past sixty, especially anyone who lived the counter-culture, this book will be an enjoyable read, and may also help to lay your own demons down.<br /><br />Thanks, Man.</span>
  <a data-text-id="5965992725711357035" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

  <div class="friendReviews elementListBrown" >
    <div class="section">
      

<div id="review_701227134" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/701227134" />
    <a title="Jack" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/855757-jack"><img alt="Jack" src="https://s.gr-assets.com/assets/nophoto/user/m_50x66-82093808bca726cb3249a493fbd3bd0f.png" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/701227134?book_show_action=true">Aug 22, 2013</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Jack" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Jack" href="/user/show/855757-jack">Jack</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="did not like it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">did not like it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer701227134" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer16578660574044447151">It's rare that I hate a protagonist this much. He's emblematic of that "my 60s generation was the best generation, man!" sort of guy. "Today's music sucks, our music was REAL ROCK! Just listen to the lyrics, man!" He's the type prone to baby boomer self-aggrandizement, but without anything to actually show for it. On one level, it seems like Martin is critiquing this 60s-Golden-Age type, but on another level I think we're supposed to buy into his impotent angst and perhaps nod along in approval </span>
  <span id="freeText16578660574044447151" style="display:none">It's rare that I hate a protagonist this much. He's emblematic of that "my 60s generation was the best generation, man!" sort of guy. "Today's music sucks, our music was REAL ROCK! Just listen to the lyrics, man!" He's the type prone to baby boomer self-aggrandizement, but without anything to actually show for it. On one level, it seems like Martin is critiquing this 60s-Golden-Age type, but on another level I think we're supposed to buy into his impotent angst and perhaps nod along in approval to his stale witticisms and toothless comebacks.<br /><br />His friends are scarcely better: we have the ex-radical hippie professor who tries to get into his students' pants, the free-love hippie mama getting in touch with motherhood and mother earth on a dippy commune, the drug-blasted hippie wastoid, and a flabby reunited hippie band it is impossible to care about. Hanging is too good for the lot of them.<br /><br />A thing we could do without: aging white authors writing about aging white authors who, despite everything, get exotic tail and just accept it like it's their due. Of course the smokin' hot black woman goes for the white author-proxy with nothing going for him, and of course it never occurs to him that this is anything more than the natural order of things. Because he's from the 60s, man.<br /><br />Another thing we could do without: Martin buts variations of the phrase "Yessa Massa" into way too many scenes. Creepy.<br /><br />The only way that this book could have been salvaged for me is if the aging hippie and all his friends had their noses rubbed in their collective generational failure, like a pack of dogs who had messed on the carpet. Or perhaps a particularly violent demise for the main character. Sadly, I got neither.</span>
  <a data-text-id="16578660574044447151" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

    <div class="section">
      

<div id="review_1393043656" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1393043656" />
    <a title="Shiloh" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/4099438-shiloh"><img alt="Shiloh" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1518645022p2/4099438.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1393043656?book_show_action=true">Sep 15, 2015</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Shiloh" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Shiloh" href="/user/show/4099438-shiloh">Shiloh</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was ok"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was ok</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4099438-shiloh?shelf=fantasy">fantasy</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4099438-shiloh?shelf=borrowed">borrowed</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4099438-shiloh?shelf=horror">horror</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1393043656" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer6892265830570680696">Martin refers to <i>The Armageddon Rag</i> as the book that almost ended his career, and after reading it, I can sort of see why. <i>The Armageddon Rag</i> is a complicated book, equal parts ode to and condemnation of the sixties and the Baby Boomers, with a healthy dose of fantasy and horror thrown in. It's a tough book to pin down, both genre-wise and attitude-wise, and I think a lot of people would find something like this confusing. Also, if you <i>are</i> a Baby Boomer, this book will probably make you mad (Mar</span>
  <span id="freeText6892265830570680696" style="display:none">Martin refers to <i>The Armageddon Rag</i> as the book that almost ended his career, and after reading it, I can sort of see why. <i>The Armageddon Rag</i> is a complicated book, equal parts ode to and condemnation of the sixties and the Baby Boomers, with a healthy dose of fantasy and horror thrown in. It's a tough book to pin down, both genre-wise and attitude-wise, and I think a lot of people would find something like this confusing. Also, if you <i>are</i> a Baby Boomer, this book will probably make you mad (Martin isn't gentle about what he sees as the failings of the generation), and if you're not, you'll probably just be confused by the references and the nostalgia. On the other hand, some of it may be familiar to those who witnessed or participated in Occupy Wall Street; while the Occupy movement never got quite as big and forward-movey as the 1960s Anti-War Hippie movement did, the root of it was the same sort of discontent and desire for change that Martin discusses in this book.<br /><br />Moving past the message a bit, I had several issues with the book. The first was the two-dimensionality of the characters. I get that they were supposed to be archetypes and maybe even allegorical figures, but a good bit of the strength of Martin's writing usually comes from his deep and nuanced characters. Even his main character was flat and generally uninteresting. Which brings me very quickly to my next problem--Ananda. She's probably the most interesting, most nuanced character in the book, and she starts a relationship with Sandy almost immediately, with no prelude or reasoning. Granted, we find out later that sex is her way of controlling him, but that's a couple of hundred pages of wondering <i>why</i> this woman would be <i>remotely</i> interested in the boring, flat Sandy.<br /><br />Closely related to that is Martin's handling of race. He doesn't exactly have the best track record with writing people of color; even as late as <i>A Dance with Dragons</i>, there are some serious problems with representation and how he writes non-white people. Ananda represents another of those problems; she is lusty, ruthless, and manipulative--and the only person of color I noticed in the book. Certainly the only one who is a main character. I don't know that I'd go so far as to call Martin a racist, but his handling of non-white characters can be distinctly troubling.<br /><br />This might be the worst of Martin's (post-fanzine) work that I've read; I may have dnf-ed <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/67956.Dying_of_the_Light" title="Dying of the Light by George R.R. Martin" rel="nofollow noopener">Dying of the Light</a>, but that wasn't because it was <i>bad</i>, just that it wasn't for me (hard sci-fi is not my genre). I think he got too passionate and preachy about the subject material, and that caused the entire book to fall really flat.</span>
  <a data-text-id="6892265830570680696" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_617075887" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/617075887" />
    <a title="V.J. Chambers" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/2497416-v-j-chambers"><img alt="V.J. Chambers" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1388183877p2/2497416.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/617075887?book_show_action=true">May 16, 2013</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="V.J. Chambers" class="user" itemprop="url" name="V.J. Chambers" href="/user/show/2497416-v-j-chambers">V.J. Chambers</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2497416-v-j-chambers?shelf=horror">horror</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer617075887" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer5658072243667641428">I wanted to love this book. In fact, I did love it, except for the ending. There are so many things about it that are just fantastic. The music, for instance. Even though you can't actually hear it, you can. You know what it would sound like it if it were real. The Nazgul themselves, sort of Black Sabbath, Led Zeppelin, and the Beatles are wrapped up into one super group. The Tolkien influences. The hints of darkness and the imagery of the end and even the Yeats stuff.<br/><br/>But it just didn't come tog</span>
  <span id="freeText5658072243667641428" style="display:none">I wanted to love this book. In fact, I did love it, except for the ending. There are so many things about it that are just fantastic. The music, for instance. Even though you can't actually hear it, you can. You know what it would sound like it if it were real. The Nazgul themselves, sort of Black Sabbath, Led Zeppelin, and the Beatles are wrapped up into one super group. The Tolkien influences. The hints of darkness and the imagery of the end and even the Yeats stuff.<br /><br />But it just didn't come together for me. This book lacks a proper climax. It sort of fizzles. The big climactic moment culminates with the main character choosing not to act, and this inaction takes all the larger-than-life characters and situations and recasts them as something mundane. The effect on the reader is sort of that we had decided to believe something bad could happen, and then, when it didn't, we feel stupid for having expected it.<br /><br />I've given this a lot of thought, and I think the only way this novel could have ended was if it were like an Ira Levin novel, or like the ending of the 70s version of Invasion of the Body Snatchers (yes, the one with Donald Sutherland.) That's the ending this book needed--a blood-dimmed tide loosed on the world, swallowing everything up, and the last sentence only an evil chuckle from Pat Hobbins. <br /><br />George R. R. Martin wussed out.<br /><br />And actually, this is beginning to worry me, because I'm afraid that perhaps Martin is not particularly good with climaxes or ending stories properly. I mean, wasn't A Song of Ice and Fire supposed to be three books long? Now, it's stretching out, further and further... Maybe he's not pumping those books out so quickly these days because he doesn't know how to end them. (And so help me, if the battle between the dragons and the Others comes down to a big non-battle like this did, I'm going to be pointing to this review and saying, "I told you so!")<br /><br />Anyway, lack of a good climax or not, I enjoyed this book, and I'd say it's worth a look.</span>
  <a data-text-id="5658072243667641428" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<span id="freeTextContainer20056959349962968">...<i>The Armageddon Rag</i> is probably the most unusual novel Martin has written. If you look at his development as a writer up to the 1980s one can only wonder what might have happened if he had continued to write novels. The fragment of <i>Black and White and Red All Over</i> that Martin published as part of the collection <i>Quartet: Four Tales from the Crossroads</i> (2001), shows that he was well on his way to delivering another very good and very different novel. One of the good things about the enormous suc</span>
  <span id="freeText20056959349962968" style="display:none">...<i>The Armageddon Rag</i> is probably the most unusual novel Martin has written. If you look at his development as a writer up to the 1980s one can only wonder what might have happened if he had continued to write novels. The fragment of <i>Black and White and Red All Over</i> that Martin published as part of the collection <i>Quartet: Four Tales from the Crossroads</i> (2001), shows that he was well on his way to delivering another very good and very different novel. One of the good things about the enormous success of <i>A Song of Ice and Fire</i> is that much of Martin's older work is back in print again (in this case despite the nightmare of getting permission from the copyright holders of several dozen songs). Each of these novels is well worth the read but personally I consider <i>The Armageddon Rag</i> the strongest of the four. Read it and expect to want to play lots of very loud music when you're done.<br /><br /><a href="http://valsrandomcomments.blogspot.com/2011/06/armageddon-rag-george-rr-martin.html" rel="nofollow noopener">Full Random Comments review</a></span>

<div id="review_1816383230" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1816383230" />
    <a title="Dane Cobain" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/19638210-dane-cobain"><img alt="Dane Cobain" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1571333550p2/19638210.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1816383230?book_show_action=true">May 04, 2020</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Dane Cobain" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Dane Cobain" href="/user/show/19638210-dane-cobain">Dane Cobain</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1816383230" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer15181560934834353695">This book’s been on my unread pile for a little while now and I’m not really sure why. I think it’s because the last time I read a GRRM book, it was the buddy read of Dying of the Light that I did with Todd the Librarian, and that wasn’t very good at all. I also knew that this particular edition has super tiny print and so that didn’t exactly endear it to me.<br/><br/>I actually didn’t know anything about this book when I picked it up, and I didn’t even notice the drum kit with the Eye of Sauron on the fr</span>
  <span id="freeText15181560934834353695" style="display:none">This book’s been on my unread pile for a little while now and I’m not really sure why. I think it’s because the last time I read a GRRM book, it was the buddy read of Dying of the Light that I did with Todd the Librarian, and that wasn’t very good at all. I also knew that this particular edition has super tiny print and so that didn’t exactly endear it to me.<br /><br />I actually didn’t know anything about this book when I picked it up, and I didn’t even notice the drum kit with the Eye of Sauron on the front cover, which probably would have given me a clue. It turns out that this book is pretty much perfect for me, because it follows a journalist investigating a series of crimes that befall the members of a 60s rock ‘n’ roll band called the Nazgul.<br /><br />For me, it was a little bit of a strange read because there are a lot of similarities between this and my own current work-in-progress, which I’ve been describing as Lord of the Rings meets Spinal Tap. Luckily, despite the fact that Martin is clearly influenced by the fantasy genre (didn’t he write some famous series or other?), he doesn’t go all in on it here, and there are no elves and dwarves knocking around like there are in my book. My book is also supposed to be funny, while Martin’s is more of a hard-boiled detective.<br /><br />That meant that I could read this one and enjoy it without having to worry too much about it putting me off from or losing faith in my own work. Better still, it brings together a whole bunch of different storytelling elements that I like and ultimately resulted in a book that was a true pleasure to read. Not bad really, considering it’s been sat on my shelves for ages and I thought I’d ran out of good stuff to read.<br /><br />I’d recommend this one without hesitation, but especially if you’re a fan of the 60s and books about music. It was almost Joe Hill-ish. Good stuff!</span>
  <a data-text-id="15181560934834353695" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2054333151" />
    <a title="Siobhan" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/33837508-siobhan"><img alt="Siobhan" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1557945251p2/33837508.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2054333151?book_show_action=true">Sep 06, 2018</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Siobhan" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Siobhan" href="/user/show/33837508-siobhan">Siobhan</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9781407247281" href="/book/show/21532697-the-armageddon-rag">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/33837508-siobhan?shelf=books-we-own-read">books-we-own-read</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2054333151" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer6185876274455623013"><b>
  <i>The best lack all conviction, whilst the worst are full of passionate intensity. </i>
</b><br/><br/>How to summarise our world in one sentence. <br/><br/>This book was deep, and I mean seriously deep. <br/><br/>It was a long and meandering journey, often purely miserable, yet it hauled me through to the finale on the edge of my seat. <br/><br/>The characters and story felt so real by the end, that I was genuinely anxious for the conclusion. The musical references throughout set the scene and drew me into nostalgia for a decade I didn't liv</span>
  <span id="freeText6185876274455623013" style="display:none"><b>
  <i>The best lack all conviction, whilst the worst are full of passionate intensity. </i>
</b><br /><br />How to summarise our world in one sentence. <br /><br />This book was deep, and I mean seriously deep. <br /><br />It was a long and meandering journey, often purely miserable, yet it hauled me through to the finale on the edge of my seat. <br /><br />The characters and story felt so real by the end, that I was genuinely anxious for the conclusion. The musical references throughout set the scene and drew me into nostalgia for a decade I didn't live through. And I want to buy the Nazgul's albums...<br /><br />An absolutely stellar book, with first class writing, that feels as relevant today as it would have done in 1983.<br /><br />4.5 stars. </span>
  <a data-text-id="6185876274455623013" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

  <span id="freeText18352056201494165492" style="display:none">You'd think that mixing GRR Martin and detective novels, which are one of my favorite genres, would be a good thing. <div id="review_32063271" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/32063271" />
    <a title="Alan" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/1153204-alan"><img alt="Alan" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1484374549p2/1153204.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/32063271?book_show_action=true">Sep 04, 2008</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Alan" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Alan" href="/user/show/1153204-alan">Alan</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Hardcover 9780671475260" href="/book/show/1281217.The_Armageddon_Rag">review of another edition</a>

          <div><span class="uitext greyText">Recommends it for: </span>
           <span class="uitext reviewText">people who wonder where we went wrong</span></div>

        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer32063271" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer16324553962371121252">The place names are pure music legend:<br/><br/>Woodstock,<br/>Altamont,<br/>Monterey,<br/>West Mesa...<br/><br/>West Mesa?<br/><br/>It is Sept. 20, 1971. The Nazgul, premier rock group of the 1960s, are performing on West Mesa near Albuquerque in front of 60,000 fans when a high-powered rifle bullet rips out the life of Patrick Henry "Hobbit" Hobbins, the Nazgul's lead singer. He dies instantly, and the 60s die with him.<br/><br/><a href='https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/67948.The_Armageddon_Rag' title='The Armageddon Rag by George R.R. Martin' rel='nofollow noopener'> The Armageddon Rag</a> begins 10 years or so later, in the decade foreshadowed by Orwell. The three surviving Nazgul have l</span>
  <span id="freeText16324553962371121252" style="display:none">The place names are pure music legend:<br /><br />Woodstock,<br />Altamont,<br />Monterey,<br />West Mesa...<br /><br />West Mesa?<br /><br />It is Sept. 20, 1971. The Nazgul, premier rock group of the 1960s, are performing on West Mesa near Albuquerque in front of 60,000 fans when a high-powered rifle bullet rips out the life of Patrick Henry "Hobbit" Hobbins, the Nazgul's lead singer. He dies instantly, and the 60s die with him.<br /><br /><a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/67948.The_Armageddon_Rag" title="The Armageddon Rag by George R.R. Martin" rel="nofollow noopener"> The Armageddon Rag</a> begins 10 years or so later, in the decade foreshadowed by Orwell. The three surviving Nazgul have long since sunk into their separate obscurities.<br /><br />Sandy Blair, aging ex-rebel and sometime reporter for the aging ex-rebel magazine <u>Hedgehog</u>, is asked to write an article for "da hog" on the bizarre sacrificial murder of Jamie Lynch, the man who had all those years ago served as the Nazgul's manager. Blair's writing career is deadlocked, and his lover can't understand his nostalgia for the 60s, but he agrees to write the article, and the enigmas Blair uncovers tie Lynch's death ever more firmly to the Nazgul and to the shadowy figure who is now trying to effect the group's comeback. Sandy's search for explanations leads him across the continent into the remnants of a 60s counterculture and closer to someone who wants to revive the Nazgul whatever the cost.<br /><br />Blair travels from one coast to the other tracking down the rest of the Nazgul: Gopher John, Rick Maggio and Peter Faxon. Along the way he encounters both old friends and old enemies, and sees a cross-section of what remains of the 60s: Froggy, the professor who still can't get tenure; Bambi, who has given up on civilization entirely; Slum, whose prison is no less real for being insubstantial; Maggie, who is drifting; and Lark, who is the most successful and hence perhaps the saddest of all.<br /><br />There appears to the author to be no way of reconciling life in the 80s with retaining one's ideals - one either compromises, like Froggy or Maggie, conforms like Lark, or withdraws like Bambi.<br /><br />There is tremendous empathy, however, for all these people. And Martin's unique blend of fantasy and reality lends his characters a great deal of credibility.<br /><br />The pace of the novel quickens as the Nazgul are reborn, and as something - perhaps the spirit of the 60s but perhaps something much more sinister - is reborn with them.<br /><br />Music pervades the book as it pervaded the era. Martin's use of lyrics from real songs of the 60s emphatically conveys a sense of the time, and the music credits in fact run for six pages. His descriptions of the Nazgul's performances and his original lyrics for such Nazgul albums as <i>Hot Wind Out Of Mordor</i> and <i>Music To Wake The Dead</i> are tremendously vivid and realistic.<br /><br />Martin's attention to detail, down to descriptions of the T-shirts sold at Nazgul concerts, adds an illusion of reality which becomes intensely surreal in the light of the magical events taking place.<br /><br /><a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/67948.The_Armageddon_Rag" title="The Armageddon Rag by George R.R. Martin" rel="nofollow noopener"> The Armageddon Rag</a> is a gripping read, well worth the price of admission to a show which will definitely "rock you till your ears bleed!"<br /><br /><br /><i>This review was one I originally got published in Marshall University's student newspaper, <u>The Parthenon</u>, on Friday, March 8, 1985. This version has been only slightly edited.</i></span>
  <a data-text-id="16324553962371121252" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_909843152" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/909843152" />
    <a title="Anna" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/8571135-anna"><img alt="Anna" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1358076399p2/8571135.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/909843152?book_show_action=true">Apr 14, 2014</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Anna" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Anna" href="/user/show/8571135-anna">Anna</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780575129559" href="/book/show/17270585-the-armageddon-rag">review of another edition</a>


        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer909843152" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer6997511837170808576"> This was my first George R. R. Martin book. Like, you know, completely written by him, not him together with Gardner Dozois. And it was quite good. I might not really be an expert in 60's music or hippie movement, but this actually made me wish the Nazgul (screw the thingies on top of the letters, meh, too tired) were a real band. They sounded so goddamn _interesting_. 60's rock might not exactly be my cup of tea, for the most part, but I wished for a soundtrack of it to go with this.<br/><br/>I started </span>
  <span id="freeText6997511837170808576" style="display:none"> This was my first George R. R. Martin book. Like, you know, completely written by him, not him together with Gardner Dozois. And it was quite good. I might not really be an expert in 60's music or hippie movement, but this actually made me wish the Nazgul (screw the thingies on top of the letters, meh, too tired) were a real band. They sounded so goddamn _interesting_. 60's rock might not exactly be my cup of tea, for the most part, but I wished for a soundtrack of it to go with this.<br /><br />I started this book when heading for Belgium, and it was a perfect traveling read. Easy to read and not too long, but with enough twists and turns to keep interest up. I managed to read about 2/3 of the book before I had actually reached my destination. Which is a good thing, that way I can finish the book before heading back home and thus start a new book on the way back (like I did now, ha!).<br /><br />I enjoyed the writing style, the characters were believable, the ideas behind the whole thing were admirable, the world slightly sad but with a hint of future promise... but somehow I was slightly disappointed at the supernatural elements in this book. They didn't ruin anything, but I still somehow was hoping it could have done what it did without them. Then again, knowing what he is known for now, I guess I should have seen that coming, eh?<br /><br />All in all, this does perk my interest in the rest of his books. For some reason I'm still more interested in the non-GoT stuff, but I guess I'll get around to reading those too, at some point later. Maybe after the tv series is done and I realize I'm way behind on everything 'hip' again. :D</span>
  <a data-text-id="6997511837170808576" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

<span id="freeTextContainer14888309157837579718"><br/>Such a weird book. <br/>I thought this would be a murder mystery mixed with some good rock music. And it was, for about 1/3 of the story. It was scary and interesting and hard to put down.<br/><br/>2/3 was about music. And it was great! I loved every single description of Nazgul on the stage, of concerts and songs - it was so real that I felt like I was right there, singing with the crowd.   <span id="freeText14888309157837579718" style="display:none"><br />Such a weird book. <br />I thought this would be a murder mystery mixed with some good rock music. And it was, for about 1/3 of the story. It was scary and interesting and hard to put down.<br /><br />2/3 was about music. And it was great! I loved every single description of Nazgul on the stage, of concerts and songs - it was so real that I felt like I was right there, singing with the crowd. Those parts were just brilliant and I enjoyed them very, very much. For some reason, I think that ''Ragin'' would be my favorite song :) <br /><br />Last third of this story went to completely different direction. It got supernatural, which I did not expect at all. It is not a bad thing and it fits to the story well, but I thihnk I would like it more if it followed the path set in the beginning. <br />By the end it got too obvious about the identity of the murderer (well at least to me because I figured it out waaay before Sandy), but then again, it wasn't even important who killed that guy anymore. It was all about Nazgul and their West Mesa concert.<br /><br />That Pat or Hobbit thing and where it took the story was a little bit too much for me. Usually I like supernatural, but I was uncomfortable with it in this book. Ending was weak. And I didn't like Sandy. <br /><br />But all in all, it was a good read. I didn't like some parts, but it was worth reading because of the music. The music deserved it 5 stars, but Sandy and his friends and the ending made me reduce my rating to 3 stars.</span>

<div id="review_1099975289" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1099975289" />
    <a title="Ell Eastwood" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/31527173-ell-eastwood"><img alt="Ell Eastwood" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1400523261p2/31527173.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1099975289?book_show_action=true">Nov 07, 2014</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Ell Eastwood" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Ell Eastwood" href="/user/show/31527173-ell-eastwood">Ell Eastwood</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was ok"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was ok</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>


          <div><span class="uitext greyText">Recommends it for: </span>
           <span class="uitext reviewText">People who read American Gods and thought &quot;I need a book that's fivehundred times worse than this&quot;</span></div>

        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/31527173-ell-eastwood?shelf=fantasy">fantasy</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/31527173-ell-eastwood?shelf=read-in-swedish">read-in-swedish</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/31527173-ell-eastwood?shelf=2014">2014</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/31527173-ell-eastwood?shelf=mystery">mystery</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1099975289" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer13027838434774463338">Torn between two and three stars. I would just LOVE to give this book five stars and say that it's a cult classic that wasn't appreciated as it should've been, because it really does have it all going for it, doesn't it? GRRM writing a mystery-fantasy novel that is a nostalgia trip to the rock music of the sixties? It should be so good, but it's not.<br/><br/>The biggest problem is the main character. OH MY GODS, how did GRRM write this shit and then go on to create all those wonderful GoT characters? I d</span>
  <span id="freeText13027838434774463338" style="display:none">Torn between two and three stars. I would just LOVE to give this book five stars and say that it's a cult classic that wasn't appreciated as it should've been, because it really does have it all going for it, doesn't it? GRRM writing a mystery-fantasy novel that is a nostalgia trip to the rock music of the sixties? It should be so good, but it's not.<br /><br />The biggest problem is the main character. OH MY GODS, how did GRRM write this shit and then go on to create all those wonderful GoT characters? I don't care if they're evil, AT LEAST THEY'RE SOMETHING. This MC is the most boring piece of shit, ever such a waste of paper to write his thoughts on. The only thing I can explain away this book with, is to say that is was some sort of method writing, where the book was written the same way the main characters writes his books.<br /><br />Because of course he's a writer. Of course he's a former hippie living with a stuck-up girlfriend who can't appreciate his need for a wild life style. She's seriously the only reasonable character in the book, I cheered like hell <a class="jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction">(view spoiler)</a><span class="spoilerContainer" style="display: none">[when they broke up <a class="jsHideSpoiler spoilerAction">(hide spoiler)</a>]</span>. And afterwards she's like "um, yeah, let me be a goddamn adult and fix this while you go wallop in self-pity you piece of trash.<br /><br />And why would you nicknamed someone called Ananda "'Nanda"? It doesn't sound good, you could just as well say Ananda because that flows from your tongue better. Sorry I just really hate nicknames that are just there because everyone needs one or something.<br /><br />All the characters are aggressively clichéd, and idk where the MC got that high horse of his, but he never gets off it and I don't understand. He's a boring dick most of the story, but for some reason he is still the choosen one (not literally) that has to save the world or whatever. I can't believe GRRM goes on about how he loves Tolkien, but that everything is too black and white in Tolkien's books when he has written THIS. WHAT WERE YOU THINKING???? (Seriously, the scenes at Slum's house, with the military dad, felt like a fucking parody, they were sooooo terrible.)<br /><br />Plus the fact that no one, not even the narrative, thinks its gross that the band - Nazgûl - commits statutory rape fucking constantly with all their groupies. I get it, that's how it goes, but it it still groooooooss.<br /><br />Reading this I'm almost (almost!) happy that the world is still a shitty capitalist place and that the hippis are dying out. Fucking finally.</span>
  <a data-text-id="13027838434774463338" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
